There he saw the face of his mother in the water, with her hand taking the offerings. Since then, many people visited the pond, hoping to see their deceased mother's face. Pilgrims believe that they will bring peace to their mothers' souls by visiting the sacred place. There are two ponds. The larger one is for ritual bathing. The smaller one is used to "look upon mother's face", and is fenced by iron bars to prevent people from bathing in it.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Traditionally, in the Kathmandu valley the South-Western corner is reserved for women and women-related rituals, and the North-Eastern is for men and men-related rituals. The worship place for Mata Tirtha Aunsi is located in Mata Tirtha in the South-Western half of the valley, while the worship place for <a href="/wiki/Gokarna_Aunsi" title="Gokarna Aunsi">Gokarna Aunsi</a>, the equivalent celebration for deceased fathers, is located in <a href="/wiki/Gokarna,_Nepal" class="mw-redirect" title="Gokarna, Nepal">Gokarna, Nepal</a>, in the North-Eastern half. This division is reflected in many aspects of the life in Kathmandu valley.<sup id="cite_ref-HeestermanHoek1992_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HeestermanHoek1992-133">[133]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Mother's Day is known as Aama ko Mukh Herne Din in <a href="/wiki/Nepali_language" title="Nepali language">Nepali</a>, which literally means "day to see mother's face". In <a href="/wiki/Nepal_Bhasa" class="mw-redirect" title="Nepal Bhasa">Nepal Bhasa</a>, the festival is known as Mām yā Khwā Swayegu, which can be translated as "to look upon mother's face".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span></h4> 
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, Mother's Day was introduced as early as 1910 by the Dutch branch of the <a href="/wiki/Salvation_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Salvation Army">Salvation Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The Royal Dutch Society for Horticulture and Botany, a group protecting the interest of Dutch florists, worked to promote the holiday; they hoped to emulate the commercial success achieved by American florists.<sup id="cite_ref-helsloot_208_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helsloot_208-135">[135]</a></sup> They were imitating the campaign already underway by florists in Germany and Austria, but they were aware that the traditions had originated in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-helsloot_208_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helsloot_208-135">[135]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Florists launched a major promotional effort in 1925. This included the publication of a book of articles written by famous intellectuals, radio broadcasts, newspapers ads, and the collaboration of priests and teachers who wanted to promote the celebration for their own reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-helsloot_208_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helsloot_208-135">[135]</a></sup> In 1931 the second Sunday of May was adopted as the official celebration date. In the mid-1930s the slogan <i>Moederdag – Bloemendag</i> (Mother's Day – Flowers' Day) was coined, and the phrase was popular for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-helsloot_209_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helsloot_209-136">[136]</a></sup> In the 1930s and 1940s "Mother's Day cakes" were given as gifts in hospitals and to the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_the_Netherlands" title="Monarchy of the Netherlands">Dutch Queen</a>, who is known as the "mother of the country".<sup id="cite_ref-helsloot_209_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helsloot_209-136">[136]</a></sup> Other trade groups tried to cash in on the holiday and to give new meaning to the holiday in order to promote their own wares as gifts.<sup id="cite_ref-helsloot_209_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helsloot_209-136">[136]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Roman Catholic priests complained that the holiday interfered with the honoring of the Virgin Mary, the divine mother, which took place during the whole month of May. In 1926 Mother's Day was celebrated on 7 July in order to address these complaints.<sup id="cite_ref-helsloot_210_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helsloot_210-137">[137]</a></sup> Catholic organizations and priests tried to Christianize the holiday, but those attempts were rendered futile around the 1960s when the church lost influence and the holiday was completely secularized.<sup id="cite_ref-helsloot_210_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helsloot_210-137">[137]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In later years, the initial resistance disappeared, and even leftist newspapers stopped their criticism and endorsed Mother's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-helsloot_213_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helsloot_213-138">[138]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 1980s, the American origin of the holiday was still not widely known, so feminist groups who opposed the perpetuation of gender roles sometimes claimed that Mother's Day was invented by Nazis and celebrated on the birthday of <a href="/wiki/Klara_Hitler" title="Klara Hitler">Klara Hitler</a>, Hitler's mother.<sup id="cite_ref-helsloot_211_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-helsloot_211-139">[139]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Zealand">New Zealand</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on the second Sunday in May. Mother's Day is not a public holiday. The New Zealand tradition is to send or give cards and or gifts and at-home youngsters to attempt to serve mothers a variation on breakfast in bed.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, the <i>Día de la Madre</i> has been celebrated on 30 May since the early 1940s. The date was chosen by President <a href="/wiki/Anastasio_Somoza_Garc%C3%ADa" title="Anastasio Somoza García">Anastasio Somoza García</a> because it was the birthday of Casimira Sacasa, his wife's mother.<sup id="cite_ref-nicaragua_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nicaragua-48">[48]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Korea">North Korea</span></h4> 
      <p>Mother's Day is celebrated on 16 November as a <a href="/wiki/List_of_public_holidays_in_North_Korea" class="mw-redirect" title="List of public holidays in North Korea">public holiday in North Korea</a>. The date takes its significance from the First National Meeting of Mothers held in 1961, for which <a href="/wiki/Kim_Il-sung" title="Kim Il-sung">Kim Il-sung</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_North_Korea" class="mw-redirect" title="Leader of North Korea">leader of the country</a>, published a work called <i>The Duty of Mothers in the Education of Children</i>. The date was designated as Mother's Day in May 2012 by the <a href="/wiki/Presidium_of_the_Supreme_People%27s_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="Presidium of the Supreme People's Assembly">Presidium of the Supreme People's Assembly</a> but only became a public holiday and appeared on the <a href="/wiki/North_Korean_calendar" title="North Korean calendar">North Korean calendar</a> starting in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-LeeSangYong2014_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeeSangYong2014-51">[51]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span></h4> 
      <p>Mother's Day was first celebrated on 9 February 1919 and was initially organized by religious institutions. Later it has become a family day, and the mother is often treated to breakfast in bed, flowers and cake.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (May 2015)">better&nbsp;source&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>It has gradually become a major commercial event, with special pastries, flowers and other presents offered by retailers. Day-cares and primary schools often encourage children to make cards and other gifts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on the second Sunday of May. Media channels celebrate with special shows. Individuals honor their mothers by giving gifts and commemorative articles. Individuals who have lost their mothers pray and pay their respects to their loved ones lost. Schools hold special programs in order to acknowledge the efforts of their mothers.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Panama">Panama</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on 8 December, the same day as the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Immaculate_Conception" title="Feast of the Immaculate Conception">Feast of the Immaculate Conception</a>. This date was suggested in 1930 by the wife of Panama's President <a href="/wiki/Florencio_Harmodio_Arosemena" title="Florencio Harmodio Arosemena">Florencio Harmodio Arosemena</a>. 8 December was adopted as Mother's Day under Law 69, which was passed the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-panama_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-panama-52">[52]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to another account, in 1924 the <a href="/wiki/Rotary_Club" class="mw-redirect" title="Rotary Club">Rotary Club</a> of Panama asked that Mother's Day be celebrated on 11 May. Politician Aníbal D. Ríos changed the proposal so that the celebration would be held on 8 December. He then established Mother's Day as a national holiday on that date.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Paraguay">Paraguay</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on 15 May, the same day as the <i>Dia de la Patria</i>, which celebrates the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Paraguay#Independence" title="History of Paraguay">independence of Paraguay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-paraguay1_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paraguay1-45">[45]</a></sup> This date was chosen to honor the role played by Juana María de Lara in the events of 14 May 1811 that led to Paraguay's independence.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2008, the Paraguayan Minister of Culture, Bruno Barrios, lamented this coincidence because, in Paraguay, Mother's Day is much more popular than independence day and the independence celebration goes unnoticed. As a result, Barrios asked that the celebration be moved to the end of the month.<sup id="cite_ref-paraguay2_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paraguay2-145">[145]</a></sup> A group of young people attempted to gather 20,000 signatures to ask the Parliament to move Mother's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-paraguay2_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paraguay2-145">[145]</a></sup> In 2008, the <i>Comisión de festejos</i> (Celebration Committee) of the city of <a href="/wiki/Asunci%C3%B3n" title="Asunción">Asunción</a> asked that Mother's Day be moved to the second Sunday of May.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span></h4> 
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, Mother's Day is officially celebrated on the second Sunday of May, but it is not a public holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-proclamation1998_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-proclamation1998-147">[147]</a></sup> Although not a traditional Filipino holiday, the occasion owes its popularity to <a href="/wiki/American_Colonial_Period_(Philippines)" class="mw-redirect" title="American Colonial Period (Philippines)">American Colonial Period</a> influence. </p>
      <p>According to a 2008 article by the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_News_Agency" title="Philippine News Agency">Philippine News Agency</a>, in 1921 the <a href="/wiki/Ilocos_Norte" title="Ilocos Norte">Ilocos Norte</a> Federation of Women's Clubs asked to declare the first Monday of December as Mother's Day "to honor these fabulous women who brought forth God's children into this world." In response, <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_the_Philippines" title="Governor-General of the Philippines">Governor-General</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Yeater" title="Charles Yeater">Charles Yeater</a> issued Circular No. 33 declaring the celebration. In 1937 <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Philippines" title="President of the Philippines">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Manuel_L._Quezon" title="Manuel L. Quezon">Manuel L. Quezon</a> issued Presidential Proclamation No. 213, changing the name of the occasion from "Mother's Day" to "Parent's Day" to address the complaints that there wasn't a "Father's Day". In 1980 President <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Marcos" title="Ferdinand Marcos">Ferdinand Marcos</a> issued Presidential Proclamation No. 2037 proclaiming the date as both Mother's Day and Father's Day. In 1988 President <a href="/wiki/Corazon_Aquino" title="Corazon Aquino">Corazon Aquino</a> issued Presidential Proclamation No. 266, changing Mother's Day to the second Sunday of May, and Father's Day to the third Sunday of June, discontinuing the traditional date.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> In 1998 President <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Estrada" title="Joseph Estrada">Joseph Estrada</a> returned both celebrations to the first Monday of December.<sup id="cite_ref-proclamation1998_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-proclamation1998-147">[147]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Portugal">Portugal</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, the "Dia da Mãe" ("Mother's Day") is an unofficial holiday held each year on the first Sunday of May (sometimes coinciding with <a href="/wiki/May_Day#Portugal" title="May Day">Labour Day</a>). The weeks leading up to this Sunday, school children spend a few hours a day to prepare a gift for their mothers, aided by their school teachers. In general, mothers receive gifts by their family members and this day is meant to be celebrated with the whole family. It used to be celebrated on 8 December, the same date of the Conception of the Virgin celebration.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Romania">Romania</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, Mother's Day has been celebrated on the first Sunday of May since 2010. Law 319/2009 made both Mother's Day and Father's Day official holidays in Romania. The measure was passed thanks to campaign efforts from the Alliance Fighting Discrimination Against Fathers (TATA).<sup id="cite_ref-romania_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-romania-38">[38]</a></sup> Previously, Mother's Day was celebrated on 8 March, as part of <a href="/wiki/International_Women%27s_Day" title="International Women's Day">International Women's Day</a> (a tradition dating back to when Romania was part of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_bloc" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern bloc">Eastern bloc</a>). Today, Mother's Day and International Women's Day are two separate holidays, with International Women's Day being held on its original date of 8 March.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_Women%27s_Day" title="International Women's Day">International Women's Day</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Traditionally <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> had celebrated International Women's Day and Mother's Day on 8 March, an inheritance from the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, and a public holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Women's Day was first celebrated on the last Sunday in February in 1913 in Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1917, demonstrations marking International Women's Day in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a> on the last Sunday in February (which fell on 8 March on the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>) initiated the <a href="/wiki/February_Revolution" title="February Revolution">February Revolution</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">October Revolution</a> later that year, the <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> <a href="/wiki/Alexandra_Kollontai" title="Alexandra Kollontai">Alexandra Kollontai</a> persuaded <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> to make it an official holiday in the Soviet Union, and it was established, but was a working day until 1965.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>On 8 May 1965, by the decree of the <a href="/wiki/Presidium_of_the_Supreme_Soviet" title="Presidium of the Supreme Soviet">Presidium of the Supreme Soviet</a>, International Women's Day was declared a non-working day in the Soviet Union "in commemoration of the outstanding merits of Soviet women in communistic construction, in the defense of their <a href="/wiki/Fatherland" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatherland">Fatherland</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Patriotic_War_(term)" title="Great Patriotic War (term)">Great Patriotic War</a>, in their heroism and selflessness at the front and in the rear, and also marking the great contribution of women to strengthening friendship between peoples, and the struggle for peace. But still, women's day must be celebrated as are other holidays."<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Samoa">Samoa</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on the second Sunday in May, and as a recognised national holiday on the Monday following. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Singapore">Singapore</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on the second Sunday of May. It is not recognized as a holiday by the government. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Slovakia">Slovakia</span></h4> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a> celebrated only Women's Day until the <a href="/wiki/Velvet_Revolution" title="Velvet Revolution">Velvet Revolution</a> in 1989. After the country split in 1993, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> started celebrating both Women's Day and Mother's Day. The politicization of Women's Day has affected the official status of Mother's Day. Center-right parties want Mother's Day to replace Women's Day, and social-democrats want to make Women's Day an official holiday. Currently, both days are festive, but they are not "state holidays". In the Slovak Republic, Mother's Day is celebrated every second Sunday in May.<sup id="cite_ref-eastern_42-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eastern-42">[42]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Africa">South Africa</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on the second Sunday of May. It is not recognized as a holiday by the government. The tradition is to give cards and gifts and to serve mothers breakfast in bed or to go out to lunch together as a family. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Sudan">South Sudan</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on the first Monday in July. The president <a href="/wiki/Salva_Kiir_Mayardit" title="Salva Kiir Mayardit">Salva Kiir Mayardit</a> proclaimed Mother's Day as the first Monday in July after handing over from Sudan. Children in South Sudan are presenting mothers with gifts and flowers. The first Mother's Day was held in that country on 2 July 2012.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span></h4> 
      <p>In Spain, Mother's Day or Día de la Madre is celebrated on the first Sunday of May. The weeks leading up to this Sunday, school children spend a few hours a day to prepare a gift for their mothers, aided by their school teachers. In general, mothers receive gifts by their family members &amp; this day is meant to be celebrated with the whole family. It is also said to be celebrated in May, as May is the month dedicated to the Virgin Mary (mother of Jesus) according to Catholicism.The idea of a month dedicated specifically to Mary can be traced back to baroque times. Although it wasn't always held during May, Mary Month included thirty daily spiritual exercises honoring Mary.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1925 the Valencian poet <a href="/w/index.php?title=Julio_Men%C3%A9ndez_Garc%C3%ADa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Julio Menéndez García (page does not exist)">Julio Menéndez García</a> published a <i>Hymn to Mother</i> in a pamphlet in which he proposed the celebration of Mother's Day in all Spanish-speaking countries.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> The official declaration was never produced, but at that time the initiative was adopted at a local level on different dates; thus, for example, in Madrid, Mother's Day was celebrated on 4 October 1926.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1939 the <a href="/wiki/Frente_de_Juventudes" title="Frente de Juventudes">Youth Front</a> of <a href="/wiki/FET_y_de_las_JONS" title="FET y de las JONS">FET y de las JONS</a> party, promoted the celebration of Mother's Day coinciding with the feast of the Immaculate Conception, on 8 December. </p>
      <p>In the early 1960s, on the initiative of a chain of department stores (<a href="/wiki/Galer%C3%ADas_Preciados" title="Galerías Preciados">Galerías Preciados</a>), which copied the custom established in Cuba, Mother's Day was also celebrated on the first Sunday of May (<a href="/wiki/El_Corte_Ingl%C3%A9s" title="El Corte Inglés">El Corte Inglés</a>, the great competitor of Galerías Preciados, celebrated the holiday in December). The two dates, May and December, coexisted until 1965 when the ecclesiastical authorities chose to celebrate the festival in May, within the month consecrated to the Virgin, to recover the authentic character of the Day of the Immaculate Conception.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sri_Lanka">Sri Lanka</span></h4> 
      <p>In Sri Lanka, Mother's Day is celebrated on the second Sunday of May. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, Mother's Day was first celebrated in 1919, by an initiative of the author Cecilia Bååth-Holmberg. It took several decades for the day to be widely recognized. Swedes born in the early nineteen hundreds typically did not celebrate the day because of the common belief that the holiday was invented strictly for commercial purposes. This was in contrast to Father's Day, which has been widely celebrated in Sweden since the late 1970s. Mother's Day in Sweden is celebrated on the last Sunday in May. A later date was chosen to allow everyone to go outside and pick flowers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Switzerland">Switzerland</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, the "règle de Pentecôte" law allows Mother's Day to be celebrated a week late if the holiday falls on the same day as Pentecost. In 2008, merchants declined to move the date.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="By_country_.28T.E2.80.93Z.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="By_country_(T–Z)">By country (T–Z)</span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Taiwan">Taiwan</span></h4> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, Mother's Day is celebrated on the second Sunday of the month of May, coinciding with <a href="/wiki/Buddha%27s_birthday" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddha's birthday">Buddha's birthday</a> and the traditional ceremony of "washing the Buddha". In 1999 the Taiwanese government established the second Sunday of May as Buddha's birthday, so they would be celebrated in the same day.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since 2006,<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Tzu_Chi" title="Tzu Chi">Tzu Chi</a>, the largest charity organization in Taiwan, celebrates the Tzu Chi Day, Mother's Day and Buddha's birthday all together, as part of a unified celebration and religious observance.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thailand">Thailand</span></h4> 
      <p>Mother's day in <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> is celebrated on the birthday of the Queen of Thailand, <a href="/wiki/Queen_Sirikit" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Sirikit">Queen Sirikit</a> (12 August).<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The holiday was first celebrated around the 1980s as part of the campaign by the Prime Minister of Thailand <a href="/wiki/Prem_Tinsulanonda" title="Prem Tinsulanonda">Prem Tinsulanonda</a> to promote Thailand's Royal family.<sup id="cite_ref-handley_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handley-165">[165]</a></sup> Father's Day is celebrated on the late King <a href="/wiki/Bhumibol_Adulyadej" title="Bhumibol Adulyadej">Bhumibol Adulyadej</a>'s birthday.<sup id="cite_ref-handley_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handley-165">[165]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ukraine">Ukraine</span></h4> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> celebrates Mother's Day (<a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a>: <span lang="uk">День Матері</span>) on the second Sunday of May. In Ukraine, Mother's Day officially became a holiday only in 1999<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> and is celebrated since 2000. Since then Ukrainian society struggles to transition the main holiday that recognizes woman from the <a href="/wiki/International_Women%27s_Day" title="International Women's Day">International Women's Day</a>, a holiday adopted under the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> that remained as a legacy in Ukraine after <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">its collapse</a>, to Mother's Day.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Happy_Mother%27s_Day_-_geograph.org.uk_-_709934.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Happy_Mother%27s_Day_-_geograph.org.uk_-_709934.jpg/170px-Happy_Mother%27s_Day_-_geograph.org.uk_-_709934.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Happy_Mother%27s_Day_-_geograph.org.uk_-_709934.jpg/255px-Happy_Mother%27s_Day_-_geograph.org.uk_-_709934.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Happy_Mother%27s_Day_-_geograph.org.uk_-_709934.jpg/340px-Happy_Mother%27s_Day_-_geograph.org.uk_-_709934.jpg 2x" data-file-width="473" data-file-height="640"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Happy_Mother%27s_Day_-_geograph.org.uk_-_709934.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Balloon" title="Balloon">Balloons</a> outside, in the week before Mother's Day 2008
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> celebrates Mother's Day on the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Sunday_in_Lent" class="mw-redirect" title="Fourth Sunday in Lent">Fourth Sunday in Lent</a> (14 March 2021).<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> In the United Kingdom, the holiday has its roots in the religious <a href="/wiki/Mothering_Sunday" title="Mothering Sunday">Mothering Sunday</a> celebration and was originally unrelated to the international Mother's Day holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-5">[5]</a></sup> Most historians believe that Mothering Sunday evolved from a medieval practice of visiting one's mother church annually on <a href="/wiki/Laetare_Sunday" title="Laetare Sunday">Laetare Sunday</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mother%27s_Day_(United_States)" title="Mother's Day (United States)">Mother's Day (United States)</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Michelle_Obama,_Jill_Biden_and_Prince_Henry_of_Wales.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Michelle_Obama%2C_Jill_Biden_and_Prince_Henry_of_Wales.jpg/220px-Michelle_Obama%2C_Jill_Biden_and_Prince_Henry_of_Wales.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Michelle_Obama%2C_Jill_Biden_and_Prince_Henry_of_Wales.jpg/330px-Michelle_Obama%2C_Jill_Biden_and_Prince_Henry_of_Wales.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Michelle_Obama%2C_Jill_Biden_and_Prince_Henry_of_Wales.jpg/440px-Michelle_Obama%2C_Jill_Biden_and_Prince_Henry_of_Wales.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4096" data-file-height="2731"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Michelle_Obama,_Jill_Biden_and_Prince_Henry_of_Wales.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Prince_Harry" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Harry">Prince Harry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Obama" title="Michelle Obama">Michelle Obama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jill_Biden" title="Jill Biden">Jill Biden</a> helping children create Mother's Day cards at the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>, 9 May 2013
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Mother%27s_day_gifts.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Mother%27s_day_gifts.jpg/220px-Mother%27s_day_gifts.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Mother%27s_day_gifts.jpg/330px-Mother%27s_day_gifts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Mother%27s_day_gifts.jpg/440px-Mother%27s_day_gifts.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2424" data-file-height="1752"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mother%27s_day_gifts.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Handmade Mother's Day gifts
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The United States celebrates Mother's Day on the second Sunday in May. In 1872 <a href="/wiki/Julia_Ward_Howe" title="Julia Ward Howe">Julia Ward Howe</a> called for women to join in support of disarmament and asked for 2 June 1872, to be established as a "Mother's Day for Peace". Her 1870 "Appeal to womanhood throughout the world" is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Mother%27s_Day_Proclamation" title="Mother's Day Proclamation">Mother's Day Proclamation</a>. But Howe's day was not for honouring mothers but for organizing pacifist mothers against war. In the 1880s and 1890s there were several further attempts to establish an American "Mother's Day", but these did not succeed beyond the local level.<sup id="cite_ref-virginia_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-virginia-169">[169]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the United States, Mother's Day remains one of the biggest days for sales of flowers, greeting cards, and the like; Mother's Day is also the biggest holiday for long-distance telephone calls.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Moreover, <a href="/wiki/Church_service" title="Church service">churchgoing</a> is also popular on Mother's Day, yielding the highest <a href="/wiki/Church_attendance" title="Church attendance">church attendance</a> after Christmas Eve and Easter. Many worshippers celebrate the day with <a href="/wiki/Carnations" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnations">carnations</a>, coloured if the mother is living and white if she is dead.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Churchgoing_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Churchgoing-171">[171]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Mother's Day continues to be one of the most commercially successful U.S. occasions.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>It is possible that the holiday would have withered over time without the support and continuous promotion of the florist industries and other commercial industries. Other Protestant holidays from the same time, such as <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Day" title="Children's Day">Children's Day</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperance_Sunday&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Temperance Sunday (page does not exist)">Temperance Sunday</a>, do not have the same level of popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> </p>

Sage Publications (CA). p.&nbsp;602. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4522-6629-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4522-6629-9"><bdi>978-1-4522-6629-9</bdi></a>. <q>She organized the first official Mother's Day service at Andrews Methodist Church in Grafton, West Virginia, on the morning of May 10, 1908. That same afternoon, 15,000 people attended a Mother's Day service at the Wanamaker Store Auditorium in Philadelphia, which she also organized. Jarvis chose the second Sunday in May for Mother's Day to mark the anniversary of her mother's death and selected her mother's favorite flower, the white carnation, as the day's official emblem.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclopedia+of+Motherhood&amp;rft.pages=602&amp;rft.pub=Sage+Publications+%28CA%29&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4522-6629-9&amp;rft.aulast=O%27Reilly&amp;rft.aufirst=Andrea&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMother%27s+Day" class="Z3988"></span></span> </li>

           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite id="CITEREFPadre_Fabián_Castro2010" class="citation news cs1">Padre Fabián Castro (3 October 2010). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://padrefabian.com.ar/el-dia-de-la-madre-en-el-mundo-y-en-la-argentina/">"El día de la madre en el mundo y en la Argentina"</a> (in Spanish). padrefabian.com.ar<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">13 May</span> 2013</span>. <q>La cuestión tiene que ver con el calendario litúrgico que la Iglesia Católica utilizaba antes de la reforma producto del Concilio Vaticano II. Allí el 11 de octubre era la festividad de la Maternidad de la Virgen María. (Actualmente se celebra el 1 de enero). Con este motivo era costumbre argentina pasar la celebración litúrgica al domingo anterior o siguiente al 11. Con el lento correr de los años la tradición popular fue fijando como el tercer domingo de octubre la celebración de la Madre y las madres.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=El+d%C3%ADa+de+la+madre+en+el+mundo+y+en+la+Argentina&amp;rft.date=2010-10-03&amp;rft.au=Padre+Fabi%C3%A1n+Castro&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fpadrefabian.com.ar%2Fel-dia-de-la-madre-en-el-mundo-y-en-la-argentina%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMother%27s+Day" class="Z3988"></span></span> </li>

DeBano (2009), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=gAR15vJcC6wC&amp;pg=PA234">"Singing against Silence: Celebrating Women and Music and the Fourth Jasmine Festival"</a>, in Laudan Nooshin (ed.), <i>Music and the Play of Power in the Middle East, North Africa and Central Asia</i>, Soas Musicology Series (illustrated&nbsp;ed.), <a href="/wiki/Ashgate_Publishing" title="Ashgate Publishing">Ashgate Publishing</a>, p.&nbsp;234 (footnote 18), <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0754634577" title="Special:BookSources/978-0754634577"><bdi>978-0754634577</bdi></a>, <q>In 2002, Fatemeh's birthday celebration (observed according to the <i>hejri</i> calendar) fell on Thursday 29 August (20 Jamādi 1423) (...) Fatemeh's birth date is also currently used to mark Mother's Day in Iran, ritually recollecting, emphasising and reinscribing her role as a loyal mother, wife, and daughter. Prior to the revolution, Mother's Day was used to promote the gender ideologies of the Pahlavi regime.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Singing+against+Silence%3A+Celebrating+Women+and+Music+and+the+Fourth+Jasmine+Festival&amp;rft.btitle=Music+and+the+Play+of+Power+in+the+Middle+East%2C+North+Africa+and+Central+Asia&amp;rft.series=Soas+Musicology+Series&amp;rft.pages=234+%28footnote+18%29&amp;rft.edition=illustrated&amp;rft.pub=Ashgate+Publishing&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.isbn=978-0754634577&amp;rft.au=Wendy+S.+DeBano&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DgAR15vJcC6wC%26pg%3DPA234&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMother%27s+Day" class="Z3988"></span></span> </li>

         <li id="cite_note-haeri1-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-haeri1_102-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite id="CITEREFShahla_Haeri1993" class="citation book cs1">Shahla Haeri (1993). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=FoF5jCEepGkC&amp;pg=PA197">"Obedience versus Autonomy: Women and Fundamentalism in Iran and Pakistan"</a>. In Martin E. Marty; R. Scott Appleby; Helen Hardacre; Everett Mendelsohn (eds.). <i>Fundamentalisms and Society: Reclaiming the Sciences, the Family, and Education</i>. The Fundamentalism Project. <b>2</b> (2&nbsp;ed.). University of Chicago Press. p.&nbsp;197. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0226508801" title="Special:BookSources/978-0226508801"><bdi>978-0226508801</bdi></a>. <q>The more women try to engage the fundamentalists in their own discourse, negotiating and bargaining over their rights (Islamic or otherwise), the more frequently has the Islamic regime emphasized the ideal, the Fatimah model, the quintessential obedient woman. The fundamentalist regime in Iran has yet to resolve its central dilemma regarding the role of women and male-female relationships: should women emulate a Zainab – autonomous and assertive – or a Fatimah – obedient and submissive? Given the logic of an Islamic marriage and the worldview it implies, the fundamentalist regime has shown a marked preference for the latter. Thus Woman's Day and Mother's Day in Iran are celebrated on the occasion of Fatimah's birth.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Obedience+versus+Autonomy%3A+Women+and+Fundamentalism+in+Iran+and+Pakistan&amp;rft.btitle=Fundamentalisms+and+Society%3A+Reclaiming+the+Sciences%2C+the+Family%2C+and+Education&amp;rft.series=The+Fundamentalism+Project&amp;rft.pages=197&amp;rft.edition=2&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Chicago+Press&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft.isbn=978-0226508801&amp;rft.au=Shahla+Haeri&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DFoF5jCEepGkC%26pg%3DPA197&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMother%27s+Day" class="Z3988"></span></span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-Sabet-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Sabet_103-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Sabet_103-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite id="CITEREFFiroozeh_Kashani-Sabet2011" class="citation cs2">Firoozeh Kashani-Sabet (2011), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=xbbT0Rsc6EMC&amp;pg=PA202"><i>Conceiving Citizens: Women and the Politics of Motherhood in Iran</i></a> (illustrated&nbsp;ed.), Oxford University Press, pp.&nbsp;201–206, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0195308860" title="Special:BookSources/978-0195308860"><bdi>978-0195308860</bdi></a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Conceiving+Citizens%3A+Women+and+the+Politics+of+Motherhood+in+Iran&amp;rft.pages=201-206&amp;rft.edition=illustrated&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.isbn=978-0195308860&amp;rft.au=Firoozeh+Kashani-Sabet&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DxbbT0Rsc6EMC%26pg%3DPA202&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMother%27s+Day" class="Z3988"></span></span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-haeri2-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-haeri2_104-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite id="CITEREFShahla_Haeri2009" class="citation cs2">Shahla Haeri (2009), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=KTou9VwtQfkC&amp;pg=PA137">"Women, Religion, and Political Agency in Iran"</a>, in Ali Gheissari (ed.), <i>Contemporary Iran:Economy, Society</i> (illustrated&nbsp;ed.), Oxford University Press, p.&nbsp;137, <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0195378481" title="Special:BookSources/978-0195378481"><bdi>978-0195378481</bdi></a>, <q>Such [feminist] gatherings would not have been so remarkable had they not happened against the backdrop of the regime's ceaseless effort to discourage, even harass, women activists and their supporters. Within the narrative of Islamization, the state's argument has been, all along, that such gatherings are representative of the culture of imperialism, and hence are subversive and against the public good and the moral order. Above all, the Islamic state has tried hard to co-opt women by appropriating the terminology and language: "protecting women," "respect for women," "gender complementarity." Accordingly, in order to accommodate, and yet control and channel women's movements and activitites, the state commemorates the birthday of Fatemeh, the Prophet Muhammad's daughter, as a national woman's/mother's day.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Women%2C+Religion%2C+and+Political+Agency+in+Iran&amp;rft.btitle=Contemporary+Iran%3AEconomy%2C+Society&amp;rft.pages=137&amp;rft.edition=illustrated&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.isbn=978-0195378481&amp;rft.au=Shahla+Haeri&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DKTou9VwtQfkC%26pg%3DPA137&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMother%27s+Day" class="Z3988"></span></span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite id="CITEREFMahdi,_Ali_Akbar2003" class="citation book cs1">Mahdi, Ali Akbar (2003). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20060901182436/http://go.owu.edu/~aamahdi/globalization-final.doc">"Iranian Women: Between Islamization and Globalization"</a>. <i>Iran Encountering Globalization: Problems and Prospects</i>. Ali Mohammadi. London and New York: Routledge/Curzon. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-415-30827-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-415-30827-4"><bdi>978-0-415-30827-4</bdi></a>. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.owu.edu/~aamahdi/globalization-final.doc">the original</a> <span class="cs1-format">(DOC)</span> on 1 September 2006. <q>This Shia vision of family is based on a nostalgic and idealistic notion of Imam Ali's family in which Fatima Zahra (the Prophet Mohammad's daughter) dedicated herself to both her husband and Islamic cause. Other role models for women often cited by the officials and ideologues of the IRI are Khadijah, the prophet Mohammad's wife, and Zaynab, daughter of the first Shi'i (sic) Imam Ali. In fact, the IRI [Islamic Republic of Iran] replaced the universal Mother's Day with Fatima Zahar's (sic) birthday.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Iranian+Women%3A+Between+Islamization+and+Globalization&amp;rft.btitle=Iran+Encountering+Globalization%3A+Problems+and+Prospects&amp;rft.pub=Ali+Mohammadi.+London+and+New+York%3A+Routledge%2FCurzon&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-415-30827-4&amp;rft.au=Mahdi%2C+Ali+Akbar&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.owu.edu%2F~aamahdi%2Fglobalization-final.doc&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMother%27s+Day" class="Z3988"></span></span> </li>

         <li id="cite_note-173"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-173">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Leigh, p. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=maF8mTPsJqsC&amp;pg=PA256&amp;vq=mother%27s+day&amp;hl=ca&amp;source=gbs_search_s&amp;cad=0#PPA256,M1">256</a></span> </li> 
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><b>General</b> </p>

             <li><a href="/wiki/Laylat_al-Qadr" class="mw-redirect" title="Laylat al-Qadr">Laylat al-Qadr</a> (Ramadan 27)</li>

             <li><a href="/wiki/Day_of_Arafah" title="Day of Arafah">Day of Arafah</a> (Dhu al-Hijjah 9)</li> 
             <li><b><a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Adha" title="Eid al-Adha">Eid al-Adha</a></b> (Dhu al-Hijjah 10)</li>
            </ul> 
           </div></td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2">
           <div>
            <b>Bold</b> indicates major holidays commonly celebrated in Algeria, which often represent the major celebrations of the month.

         <tr>
          <td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2" style="font-size:90%;">
           <div>
            Legend: 
            <p>(federal) = federal holidays, (abbreviation) = state/territorial holidays, (religious) = religious holidays, (cultural) = holiday related to a specific <a href="/wiki/Race_and_ethnicity_in_the_United_States_Census" title="Race and ethnicity in the United States Census">racial/ethnic group</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sexual_minority" title="Sexual minority">sexual minority</a>, (week) = week-long holidays, (month) = month-long holidays, (36) = <a href="/wiki/Title_36_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 36 of the United States Code">Title 36 Observances and Ceremonies</a><br> <b>Bold</b> indicates major holidays commonly celebrated in the United States, which often represent the major celebrations of the month.<br> </p> See also: <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_holidays" title="Lists of holidays">Lists of holidays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hallmark_holiday" title="Hallmark holiday">Hallmark holidays</a>, public holidays in the <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_the_United_States" title="Public holidays in the United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/State_holidays_in_New_Jersey" title="State holidays in New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_government_holidays" title="New York State government holidays">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Public holidays in Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_the_United_States_Virgin_Islands" title="Public holidays in the United States Virgin Islands">United States Virgin Islands</a>.
           </div></td>
         </tr>
        </tbody>
       </table>
      </div>
